<div id="review_18451945" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18451945" />
    <a title="will" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1015266-will"><img alt="will" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345397159p2/1015266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18451945?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="will" class="user" itemprop="url" name="will" href="/user/show/1015266-will">will</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18451945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14829717916987208120">[The following review was written three years ago, when I read the book the first time. I expected to change my opinion of the book, and at least take one star off my rating. However, it is a good book, and was not damaged by a second reading. Therefore, I'll let the review and the rating stand.]<br/><br/>Before I start I must say that I enjoy Robert Harris books. I picked up Fatherland in one of those "buy three books get the fourth free" promotions. I had no intention of getting it but you know how it i</span>
  <span id="freeText14829717916987208120" style="display:none">[The following review was written three years ago, when I read the book the first time. I expected to change my opinion of the book, and at least take one star off my rating. However, it is a good book, and was not damaged by a second reading. Therefore, I'll let the review and the rating stand.]<br /><br />Before I start I must say that I enjoy Robert Harris books. I picked up Fatherland in one of those "buy three books get the fourth free" promotions. I had no intention of getting it but you know how it is, you can never find the right amount of books on the promotion table, so I picked it up. I was hooked. I then read Archangel and loved it. Since then I've managed to read through all his other books and haven't been disappointed. Thus it was that, when I read on neil h.'s blog, there was a new Harris book out I immediately added it to my mental "to buy" list. However, that was six (?) months ago and somehow I failed to get - mainly because of the cost, it was only available in hardback and when the choice comes down to buying one hardback or two paperbacks the latter always wins. Imagine my disappointment when I arrived in Borders with money to spend to discover that they didn't have a copy of it - it was immense. We left Borders with a box of books and I mentioned that I had picked up the new Roddy Doyle book - that was a sequel to a previous book he had written. Maria left me to put the books in the car and immediately went back into the shop to by "The Woman Who Walked Into Doors". I put the books in the car, lit a cigarette and hung around outside the shop waiting for her to re-emerge. As I stood waiting my eyes travelled across the bargain bins outside Borders and there, waving at me, was a copy of Imperium. I was sorted!<br /><br />Recently we have watched the excellent Rome - both series - and we loved it. Here is a book that fleshes out some of the story. It is basically a telling of Cicero's life, his rise through the political machinations, starting as a simple senator until arriving at the point of Consul (or Imperium). Robert Harris manages to tell a fascinating story full of intrigue. I like history and one of the reasons I like the subject so much is that it is possible to see parallels in the modern day. Is Cicero a Tony Blair? Full of promises and offers of salvation to the common people who eventually sells out to the old order - does the "new man" end up becoming the type of man who he despised? Is it possible to seek power and still remain "honest"? Honest to your beliefs and just honest?<br /><br />I really enjoyed the book. It is one of those books that you put down and wish that the author is now working on a sequel. Oh, I know what happened to Julius Caesar but I want him to tell me how he thinks it all went on. Considering that Mr. Harris's last book before this was Pompeii, I can but hope that he is stuck in that era (except that having checked Amazon it appears he already has a new book out - and it is set in the present day. Bum!) </span>
  <a data-text-id="14829717916987208120" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2099517662" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2099517662" />
    <a title="Bubu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44712859-bubu"><img alt="Bubu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510872559p2/44712859.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2099517662?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bubu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bubu" href="/user/show/44712859-bubu">Bubu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="MP3 Book 9781408423684" href="/book/show/9973151-imperium">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44712859-bubu?shelf=read-2017">read-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44712859-bubu?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44712859-bubu?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44712859-bubu?shelf=2-stars">2-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2099517662" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9483044820495551058"><b>1.5 stars for the narration, 4 for the story. But I'm rounding it down because of the awful audio version.</b><br/><br/>For a more in-depth analysis of the book, please read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1927872826?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Marquise's</a> wonderfully written review.<br/><br/>Robert Harris' books are pretty much hit and miss for me. But this series is truly good.<br/><br/><b>Imperium</b> tells the story of Marcus Tullius Cicero, advocate, politician and orator; his rise to power as well as the slow decline of the Roman Republic. It's captivating and well written, giving the reader a fairly</span>
  <span id="freeText9483044820495551058" style="display:none"><b>1.5 stars for the narration, 4 for the story. But I'm rounding it down because of the awful audio version.</b><br /><br />For a more in-depth analysis of the book, please read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1927872826?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Marquise's</a> wonderfully written review.<br /><br />Robert Harris' books are pretty much hit and miss for me. But this series is truly good.<br /><br /><b>Imperium</b> tells the story of Marcus Tullius Cicero, advocate, politician and orator; his rise to power as well as the slow decline of the Roman Republic. It's captivating and well written, giving the reader a fairly historically correct recount and analysis of events that leads to Cicero's consulship. Told by Cicero's former house slave and secretary, Tiro, it maintains a distance to the characters that I prefer in Historical Fiction.<br /><br />Equally interesting is the glimpse we get of the structure of the Roman Republic. I believe it's safe to say that Hollywood has been successful in reducing this time period to sandals-and-swords-gladiator narratives. <br /><br />Unfortunately, the audio version doesn't reflect the fascinating mechanisms, power struggles and intrigues that Robert Harris so aptly put into words. If I hadn't already read the books years ago, thus remembering roughly who's who and what's what, I would have struggled to stay interested. <br /><br />Considering that Cicero was the greatest orator of his time, the narrator never gave Cicero's voice the immense power he wielded with his speeches which is an utter shame. Speeches could make or break a Roman politician in those times, and Robert Harris gave Cicero's speeches the necessary background and build-up to make it an engrossing read.<br /><br />For anyone interested: read the books and avoid the audio version. Or, at least, listen to the sample first. The books are really good.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9483044820495551058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_8890940" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/8890940" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/216284-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1254348059p2/216284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8890940?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/216284-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/216284-mark?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8890940" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1928827053464712455">I loved this book. Harris has written a novel that combines a good political potboiler with solid historical fiction, based on real events in the life of the famous Roman senator and consul Cicero.<br/><br/>Narrated by Cicero's slave and scribe, Tiro, who invented an early elaborate version of shorthand so he could take down speeches and debates as they occurred, the novel joins Cicero as a young man, and then takes us through his landmark prosecution of Verres, a corrupt governor of Sicily (and you wonde</span>
  <span id="freeText1928827053464712455" style="display:none">I loved this book. Harris has written a novel that combines a good political potboiler with solid historical fiction, based on real events in the life of the famous Roman senator and consul Cicero.<br /><br />Narrated by Cicero's slave and scribe, Tiro, who invented an early elaborate version of shorthand so he could take down speeches and debates as they occurred, the novel joins Cicero as a young man, and then takes us through his landmark prosecution of Verres, a corrupt governor of Sicily (and you wondered how early the Mafia got its roots), his alliance with the general Pompey and his lifelong opposition to another wealthy general, Crassus, and his closely contested election as the first and only consul of Rome who was not an aristocrat, a general or someone who bribed his way into office.<br /><br />The book is packed with colorful details about the ancient city of Rome, its political system, its domestic arrangements, its homes, and the ancient rivalry between the patrician families and the "new men" who came from a lower class, like Cicero.<br /><br />It also gives a fascinating early glimpse of Julius Caesar, who is a young, ambitious politician in this novel. Since this is apparently intended to be part one of a trilogy, I have a feeling we'll see the general and emperor Caesar later on (although Harris took time out to write a modern day political thriller this year called "Ghost"). <br /><br />Harris has written earlier novels about Pompeii and about events during World War II. He is, at bottom, a damned good storyteller and a more than creditable writer. Dan Brown could wish to be this good on one of Harris' bad days.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1928827053464712455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2324170069" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2324170069" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2324170069?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jess The Bookworm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jess The Bookworm" href="/user/show/32042916-jess-the-bookworm">Jess The Bookworm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2324170069" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16165761375910391747">This novel follows the life of Cicero, the famous Roman Consul who was well-known for being one of the greatest orators of all time. It covers the life of Cicero up until he's elected as Consul of Rome, and tells of his rise to political power through the ranks, including his successes as a lawyer, including his trial against Verres, the corrupt Roman governor. <br/><br/>I think that I found this book most interesting as I am a lawyer myself, and of course in first year University I had to study the found</span>
  <span id="freeText16165761375910391747" style="display:none">This novel follows the life of Cicero, the famous Roman Consul who was well-known for being one of the greatest orators of all time. It covers the life of Cicero up until he's elected as Consul of Rome, and tells of his rise to political power through the ranks, including his successes as a lawyer, including his trial against Verres, the corrupt Roman governor. <br /><br />I think that I found this book most interesting as I am a lawyer myself, and of course in first year University I had to study the foundations of South African law, being Roman Law. I also find ancient Rome fascinating in general. It is weirdly comforting to know that politics and politicians have always been corrupt and that it's not just our government of today. Somehow the world keeps turning, from one bad government to the next.<br /><br />I did enjoy that this book was written in a very easy to read format, and even though I thought that it might be a daunting novel, it was in fact not as academic as I first feared. Cicero is a very interesting historical figure and I'm glad that I found this series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16165761375910391747" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_357944645" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/357944645" />
    <a title="Veeral" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6969151-veeral"><img alt="Veeral" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1344326461p2/6969151.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/357944645?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Veeral" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Veeral" href="/user/show/6969151-veeral">Veeral</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099527664" href="/book/show/6511526-imperium">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6969151-veeral?shelf=favorites-of-2012">favorites-of-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6969151-veeral?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6969151-veeral?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer357944645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15136027967139226467">I have read three books by Robert Harris recently; this one, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/880.Pompeii' title='Pompeii by Robert Harris' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pompeii</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/843989.Enigma' title='Enigma by Robert Harris' rel='nofollow noopener'>Enigma</a>. Notwithstanding anything I might have found previously to say about his works, one thing I have to give him is that the man does his research quiet well. He does not rely on heavy words like some authors to veil an otherwise paper thin plot (that is, if there is one to start with), but he trusts his immaculate research to speak for his work with a clean narration. As a result, the reader is exposed to a very well re</span>
  <span id="freeText15136027967139226467" style="display:none">I have read three books by Robert Harris recently; this one, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/880.Pompeii" title="Pompeii by Robert Harris" rel="nofollow noopener">Pompeii</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/843989.Enigma" title="Enigma by Robert Harris" rel="nofollow noopener">Enigma</a>. Notwithstanding anything I might have found previously to say about his works, one thing I have to give him is that the man does his research quiet well. He does not rely on heavy words like some authors to veil an otherwise paper thin plot (that is, if there is one to start with), but he trusts his immaculate research to speak for his work with a clean narration. As a result, the reader is exposed to a very well researched work with normal and light prose, which as an after-effect keeps the migraine situation under control.<br /><br />For Imperium, his principal source of material was the twenty-nine volumes of Cicero’s speeches and letters as he himself points out in his acknowledgement. And his effort paid off (for me, at least).<br /><br />Imperium is an imaginary biography of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cicero" rel="nofollow noopener">Marcus Tullius Cicero</a>, Rome’s first and greatest politician. The book is narrated by Tiro, Cicero’s slave and Secretary, and apparently the inventor of the art of shorthand, which made it possible to take down fully and correctly the words of public speakers, however rapid their enunciation. Unfortunately, Cicero’s real biography written by Tiro was lost to history as the Roman Empire fell gradually.<br /><br />Cicero, against daunting odds and even mightier rivals, without any substantial backing, decided to become the consul of Roman Empire (the highest post in the senate) with his wit, dare and intelligence. What follows is the account of his eventual achievement and even though you know what is going to happen, the book compels you to keep reading, which sounds easier but is way harder for an author to accomplish. <br /><br />Meanwhile Harris does not forget to offer some fantabulous quotes in his narration, for example,<br /><br /><i>“Politics is a country idiot, and capable of concentrating on only one thing at a time.”<br /><br />“If it is gratitude you want, get a dog.”</i> <br /><br />The above quote by Cicero is because his brother asks him that why Pompey should not support Cicero in his ambition for becoming consul as he should be grateful for what Cicero did for him.<br /><br /><i>“Power brings a man many luxuries, but a clean pair of hands is seldom among them.”</i><br /><br />The magnitude of Marcus Tullius Cicero's accomplishment could be summed up "easily" with the following words that: at forty-two, the youngest age allowable, he became the supreme Imperium of the Roman consulship and as a “new man,” without family, fortune, or force of arms to assist him: a feat never accomplished before or afterwards in Roman History.<br /><br />It is really baffling to read how the politics of Roman era is reminiscent to the modern day politics. For better or worse, so little has changed. And also, Cicero reminded me of the pioneer of Indian politics, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chanakya" rel="nofollow noopener">Chanakya</a>, whose feat I consider second to none, who, as a matter of fact, was born 164 years before Cicero. (Although they both served different political systems). <br /><br />Yes the book plods somewhat especially in the second half but considering the subject of the novel, I don’t think any other author could have done better than what Robert Harris did.<br /><br />So, a very well deserved <b>3.5 stars.</b><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15136027967139226467" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1255879184" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1255879184" />
    <a title="Hannah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6940509-hannah"><img alt="Hannah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564833262p2/6940509.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1255879184?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah" href="/user/show/6940509-hannah">Hannah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1255879184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5255904609086167265"><b>4 Stars</b> - Great book<br/><br/><b>The one or two spoilers are not hidden because they are mainly historical facts that anyone could Google. You've been warned.</b><br/><br/>I have a passion for/deep interest in ancient history and ancient cultures, Ancient Rome is my favorite of those ancient cultures. I love that a culture and history so old is as well documented as it is (comparatively speaking) with its literature, poetry, buildings, and monuments. This fascination started when I took Latin in high school. Yes, I took L</span>
  <span id="freeText5255904609086167265" style="display:none"><b>4 Stars</b> - Great book<br /><br /><b>The one or two spoilers are not hidden because they are mainly historical facts that anyone could Google. You've been warned.</b><br /><br />I have a passion for/deep interest in ancient history and ancient cultures, Ancient Rome is my favorite of those ancient cultures. I love that a culture and history so old is as well documented as it is (comparatively speaking) with its literature, poetry, buildings, and monuments. This fascination started when I took Latin in high school. Yes, I took Latin all 4 years and no, it wasn't a requirement. I chose to take it. I thoroughly enjoyed the way <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/575.Robert_Harris" title="Robert Harris" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert Harris</a> created this historical fiction work of art. I liked what he did with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/880.Pompeii" title="Pompeii by Robert Harris" rel="nofollow noopener">Pompeii</a> and wanted to read another by him.<br /><br />The story centers around Marcus Tullius Cicero, Roman lawyer, politician, and (probably best known as) orator. Set between 79 and 64 BCE Harris tells the story of Cicero's rise in Roman politics and his rise to <i>Imperium</i> - specific and coveted type of power and prestige in Ancient Rome. The story is told through the eyes of Cicero's trusted secretary and slave, Tiro. Harris states that Tiro did actually write a life of Cicero (he uses Plutarch and Asconius as his sources for this and those are pretty good sources if you ask me), but it was lost in the fall of Rome. Harris draws on actual events and creates a fascinating political thriller.<br /><br />This book is about Ancient Rome, yes, but it also about politics. I would go so far as to say it's first and foremost about politics. And I know, if you're anything like me, that in this day-and-age politics can make you groan and roll your eyes at the very least. Every time I receive an alert from one of my news apps on my phone I usually have a strong desire to punch the nearest wall, after reading what it says. However I loved the politics of this book mainly because I love this era in history and I didn't have to live through it (that always makes things easier). We get to read about the well-known Pompey, Crassus, and even Caesar through the eyes of Cicero's slave; I'd even say that we see it from Cicero's eyes as well because of how close Harris describes the relationship between Cicero and Tiro.<br /><br />The story is absolutely fascinating. The book ends with Cicero's election to consul which was his ultimate goal. However as anyone familiar with Roman history knows, his election to counsel precedes the fall of the Republic and Caesar's rise to power. The ending, and this is one of my favorite parts of this book, alludes to the fact that even though Cicero won consul there were tougher times ahead. And anyone who knows about this history already knows that but it's executed so beautifully in this book.<br /><br />I recommend this book for people who like historical fiction, Ancient Rome, politics, and/or drama. I look forward to reading more by this author. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5255904609086167265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12069143367134844867">I really enjoyed this! That's despite the fact that it was about Cicero, who the historical record clearly shows was a dick. One of the best parts of Harris's writing was how well he was able to sketch character portraits of the dozens of characters included, particularly Cicero and the other senators. Cicero is a true politician: he's in this for the consulship, and viewed through that lens (and supported by his writings), he becomes a real, fleshed-out person who I couldn't quite love but did </span>
  <span id="freeText12069143367134844867" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this! That's despite the fact that it was about Cicero, who the historical record clearly shows was a dick. One of the best parts of Harris's writing was how well he was able to sketch character portraits of the dozens of characters included, particularly Cicero and the other senators. Cicero is a true politician: he's in this for the consulship, and viewed through that lens (and supported by his writings), he becomes a real, fleshed-out person who I couldn't quite love but did root for. The others, despite less page time, get similar treatment too. The few moments when Caesar appears on the page describe a charming megalomaniac <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(Caesar and Mucia!!! I'm scarred!!) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, and Pompey was just blustery enough. Harris has a gift for bringing these historical figures alive. I may have liked his writing even more than Robert Graves's. I WENT THERE.<br /><br />Also, I love good Roman historical fiction when it gives me tidbits about life in the era, and this book delivered in spades. Best moment: Tiro describes the pigeons who roost in the Senate house and who start fluttering around when there's a commotion below. The superstitious senators have differing opinions on whether excrement landing on them is a good or bad omen. <br /><br />I'm definitely going to read the rest of the series. This is what I wish the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/514099842" rel="nofollow noopener">Colleen McCullough</a> books were like!</span>

<div id="review_1811698254" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1811698254" />
    <a title="Roman Clodia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia"><img alt="Roman Clodia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464021031p2/56111743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1811698254?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roman Clodia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roman Clodia" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia">Roman Clodia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11797614-imperium">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1811698254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11172496678673616786">Uneven and meandering, lacking a strong historical sense<br/><br/>This is an ok rather than a brilliant read. I found it unevenly written and paced, and while the plot starts quite tight it ends up meandering all over as if it's not quite sure what it wants to be about.<br/><br/>It starts off being about Cicero and the Verres scandal (if you're interested in the actual speeches that Cicero made against Verres, they can be found in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6421071.Political_Speeches' title='Political Speeches by Marcus Tullius Cicero' rel='nofollow noopener'>Political Speeches</a>), but then turns into Cicero's chase for the consulship.<br/><br/>There isn'</span>
  <span id="freeText11172496678673616786" style="display:none">Uneven and meandering, lacking a strong historical sense<br /><br />This is an ok rather than a brilliant read. I found it unevenly written and paced, and while the plot starts quite tight it ends up meandering all over as if it's not quite sure what it wants to be about.<br /><br />It starts off being about Cicero and the Verres scandal (if you're interested in the actual speeches that Cicero made against Verres, they can be found in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6421071.Political_Speeches" title="Political Speeches by Marcus Tullius Cicero" rel="nofollow noopener">Political Speeches</a>), but then turns into Cicero's chase for the consulship.<br /><br />There isn't a strong sense of Republican Rome and this could have been a contemporary novel rather than 'historical' as far as atmosphere went. Rather oddly, Harris mixes up contemporary with historical so, for example, he talks about Cicero's 'drawing room', his 'valet', and describes men with 'pomaded hair' in the Forum! Also his understanding of the political structure is innaccurate: no-one was ever voted in as a senator: they stood for political office (as a quaestor, aedile, praetor) and automatically joined the senate as a result of winning; and his use of 'imperium' is also not quite right: it doesn't mean 'power' but a specific form of authority i.e. consuls had imperium and so did an army commander but he only held it outside the city and laid it down before crossing into the city of Rome. These might be niggles but I found them very off-putting and irritating.<br /><br />Harris also simplifies the politics of this period and, very oddly, makes Cicero something quite different from the historical record: Harris' Cicero is constantly described as being on the side of the 'radicals' as a 'revolutionary', as a man of the people and against the 'aristocrats'. Apart from Roman society not really being split along such simple lines, Cicero was never a radical in the slightest. As a 'new man' (novus hominus) he was desperate to be part of the traditional elite and was against the men who did want to change society legitimately such as Caesar.<br /><br />So I did enjoy this in parts as I love this period, but objectively speaking this just isn't that good a book. For a far better fictional evocation of ancient Rome, stronger characters, and a real sense of history I would recommend Colleen McCullough's Masters of Rome series far above this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11172496678673616786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_671831067" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/671831067" />
    <a title="Brad Lyerla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4384303-brad-lyerla"><img alt="Brad Lyerla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599034073p2/4384303.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/671831067?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad Lyerla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad Lyerla" href="/user/show/4384303-brad-lyerla">Brad Lyerla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer671831067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer920143255539400566">IMPERIUM recounts the story of Marcus Cicero's ascent from modest young lawyer to the leading advocate of the late Roman Republic and one of the greatest orators of all time. IMPERIUM is the first of a three volume set. It concludes with Cicero overcoming treachery to win an ugly election and become leader of the Roman senate. <br/><br/><br/>Cicero's story during this period is fascinating. Circumstances compel him, against his better judgment, to make an alliance with Pompey the Great which alienates him from</span>
  <span id="freeText920143255539400566" style="display:none">IMPERIUM recounts the story of Marcus Cicero's ascent from modest young lawyer to the leading advocate of the late Roman Republic and one of the greatest orators of all time. IMPERIUM is the first of a three volume set. It concludes with Cicero overcoming treachery to win an ugly election and become leader of the Roman senate. <br /><br /><br />Cicero's story during this period is fascinating. Circumstances compel him, against his better judgment, to make an alliance with Pompey the Great which alienates him from the hugely wealthy Crassus and the demagogue, Julius Ceasar. Together, Crassus and Ceasar are very formidable adversaries. Cicero wins the election, but the die is cast and his future in politics promises to be perilous as he battles to preserve the Republic against historical forces that are undermining Rome's democratic institutions and pushing Rome toward dictatorship. This is compelling stuff and a fun read.</span>

Both these books from my favorite authors are endless summary, endless telling with little showing, and endless, endless, impenetrable Roman names, hundreds of characters, most of whom mean nothing.<br/>I think Harris' problem is, in writing about the real Cicero and what really happened, he felt constrained to tell us all the boring crap that is true, and fail</span>
  <span id="freeText2996713331445873047" style="display:none">Robert Harris has been replaced by an alien doppelganger, probably the same alien who wrote Iron and Rust, pretending to be Harry Sidebottom. Both these books from my favorite authors are endless summary, endless telling with little showing, and endless, endless, impenetrable Roman names, hundreds of characters, most of whom mean nothing.<br />I think Harris' problem is, in writing about the real Cicero and what really happened, he felt constrained to tell us all the boring crap that is true, and failed to show us any excitement or tension which might not be true. This from the man who wrote Fatherland, a novel where the Nazis win WW II and rule all of Europe, and Joe Kennedy is president of the U.S.<br />I suffered through this dreadful book because all of his others are such stellar thrillers. Ancient Rome is fascinating and it takes a special talent to make it so pedantic. </span>

<div id="review_2841182609" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2841182609" />
    <a title="Justine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71147186-justine"><img alt="Justine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514980922p2/71147186.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2841182609?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justine" href="/user/show/71147186-justine">Justine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782259203869" href="/book/show/35793709-imperium">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71147186-justine?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2841182609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2747830691459275873">Maybe you don't know, but I love the Ancient times, be them Greek, Roman or other, and I always look for books dealing with/set in this period! So, when a friend lent me <i>Imperium</i>, I nearly read it right away! <br/><br/>Moreover, just like some other Latin students, I had to translate extracts from Cicero's letters and speeches. So, I can’t say I was especially fond of him before reading this book. That’s the first exploit of <i>Imperium</i>: it made me love Cicero. Really love him; like I supported him from begi</span>
  <span id="freeText2747830691459275873" style="display:none">Maybe you don't know, but I love the Ancient times, be them Greek, Roman or other, and I always look for books dealing with/set in this period! So, when a friend lent me <i>Imperium</i>, I nearly read it right away! <br /><br />Moreover, just like some other Latin students, I had to translate extracts from Cicero's letters and speeches. So, I can’t say I was especially fond of him before reading this book. That’s the first exploit of <i>Imperium</i>: it made me love Cicero. Really love him; like I supported him from beginning to end – even if I already knew some events, it was really captivating, even suspenseful at times! – and I now want to read the sequel, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3629971.Conspirata__Cicero___2_" title="Conspirata (Cicero, #2) by Robert Harris" rel="nofollow noopener">Conspirata</a>
</i>! I want to read more about him, even if I know the outlines of his life. I want to follow him until his downfall and death! – I must admit now that I don’t remember how he died, so, it might be a bit of a surprise! What was particularly great about following Cicero was that, even if he is not the narrator, we get to see some of his feelings; it feels like we really get to know him, a bit like Hadrian in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12172.Memoirs_of_Hadrian" title="Memoirs of Hadrian by Marguerite Yourcenar" rel="nofollow noopener">Memoirs of Hadrian</a>
</i> by Marguerite Yourcenar – except that, in this book, Hadrian is the narrator, whereas Cicero is not. <br /><br />Which leads me to another thing I particularly loved here: the way the book is written. Cicero’s life is told by his former slave, Tiro. And we can feel the love he feels for his master; and, so, through his eyes, the reader grows attached to Cicero and his family! The author took this idea from the fact that Tiro actually wrote Cicero’s biography, but it was lost. Thanks to this perspective, emotion blooms, and, once more, just like with Hadrian, we feel close to him, and sympathize with him. Beware, this spoiler is about both <i>Imperium</i> and <i>Memoirs of Hadrian</i>! <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Lucius’s death made me think of Antiochus’: it was terribly sad to read, and to see the characters’ grief! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />This book is also a delight for those who love to discover more about Roman mores - the importance of family for instance -, and, mostly, Roman politics. Cicero wants to climb the social ladder, meaning in Rome, the political ladder. We follow his campaigns, but also his work as a lawyer; we get some extracts for his speeches, and we can only imagine what it must have felt like to hear him during a trial! I really wouldn't have liked to be his enemy in court! In the beginning of <i>Imperium</i>, Tiro describes Cicero as a voice, the most famous voice in the world, and it was great to "hear" it here, through Tiro's words. <br />Politics also means corruption, and there are many examples of that in this book! Cicero is not immune to it but, for the most part, he fights against it - he has no choice as he is an <i>homo novus</i>, meaning that his family isn't known in the political or social world. <br />I loved to see other great historical characters, such as Pompeius, Caesar or Crassus; other characters are known thanks to Cicero and his famous trials, such as Catilina <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[but the biggest part has yet to come! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> This book was, basically, a treat for me! <br /><br />Finally, I loved the writing style! I rarely do that, but I read this book in translation (in French), and I must admit it didn’t read like a translated work! It was fluent, and I wrote down some passages that particularly spoke to me. It was beautiful, and made me want to discover other books by Robert Harris! I still want to reread <i>Imperium</i> in English though! <br /><br />The only thing that bothered me - but it can't be helped when you write about Ancient Rome it seems - is the Romans' misogyny. Like Tiro saying that the system he invented to write things down super quickly is so simple that even a woman could do it. Haha, great. But, the author made a great job portraying Terentia: she is a strong woman, her husband listens to her - he must, she holds the purse strings and he needs money to get into politics!! I grew attached to her, even if the first impression is not the best through Tiro's eyes! <br /><br /><br />So, this discovery was a success: I’m glad I read a new great book set in Ancient Rome, glad I discovered new fictional and historical characters to love, and a new author to read more from! I really didn’t expect this book to be this good, I really had a great time! </span>
  <a data-text-id="2747830691459275873" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_226889430" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/226889430" />
    <a title="Blair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/272909-blair"><img alt="Blair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493499323p2/272909.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/226889430?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blair" href="/user/show/272909-blair">Blair</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11797614-imperium">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=2000s-release">2000s-release</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=read-on-kindle">read-on-kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer226889430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11521346334601682842">I love reading about ancient Rome. It's a period I have become very interested in and learned about primarily though fiction (films as well as books). I have also enjoyed the Robert Harris books I've read in the past, which, although certainly intelligent and well-written, have been fast-paced, dramatic and quick reads. Thus while the Roman setting of <i>Imperium</i> intrigued me, I expected it to be light and easy to read, and probably heavily embellished. In actual fact, it's really quite a serious n</span>
  <span id="freeText11521346334601682842" style="display:none">I love reading about ancient Rome. It's a period I have become very interested in and learned about primarily though fiction (films as well as books). I have also enjoyed the Robert Harris books I've read in the past, which, although certainly intelligent and well-written, have been fast-paced, dramatic and quick reads. Thus while the Roman setting of <i>Imperium</i> intrigued me, I expected it to be light and easy to read, and probably heavily embellished. In actual fact, it's really quite a serious novel; lengthy, detailed and impeccably well-researched. The first of a trilogy, it stays close to what is known of real-life events in the life of Roman lawyer and statesman Cicero. The tale is narrated by Cicero's trusted secretary, Tiro, whose voice gives the book a welcome warmth and allows for personal observations which really bring the story to life. This volume is an account of Cicero's ascent to power as consul of Rome, a journey fraught with backstabbing, vituperation and several uneasy alliances.<br /> <br />Reading this so soon after Harris's contemporary thriller <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3156040.The_Ghost" title="The Ghost by Robert Harris" rel="nofollow noopener">The Ghost</a>, widely believed to have been inspired by Tony Blair's political career, I couldn't help but notice the very modern spin Harris has put on the political affairs of the time. When an attack is mounted by a group of pirates, there are clear parallels with the 'war on terror', with Pompey as a power-crazed George Bush figure keen to fight back with disproportionate military force. Cicero himself has echoes of New Labour; while a 'man of the people' who is derided by the aristocrats because of his comparatively humble birth and support for wider democracy, he is chiefly a fiercely ambitious individual. Determined to achieve supreme political power - the 'imperium' of the title - at any cost, he is perfectly prepared to make alliances with his bitterest enemies and support undemocratic decisions. Political intrigue is constantly bubbling under, and in fact there is little else to the story; there's virtually no sex and only a handful of violent scenes. There are, however, appearances by a great number of significant historical figures, notably Pompey, Crassus and a young Caesar.<br /><br />Because the book is necessarily written in a modern style, there are points when it seems a little inauthentic, for all its attention to historical detail. There are some notably incongruous words - 'quiff', 'dandy' and 'highfalutin' particularly stood out to me. At times the opposite is true; the factual descriptions of Roman public life can drag the narrative down, making it feel a little too dry. For most of the book, though, Harris balances fact and fiction effectively, bringing Rome to life with evocative, but restrained, prose.<br /><br />Vivid and absorbing, this novel not only held my attention, but also made me feel I'd learned quite a lot about this particular period in history, and about how Roman democracy operated. It's not always a page-turner, and may not appeal to everyone who has enjoyed Harris's contemporary fiction. However, I really enjoyed spending time in the company of Tiro and Cicero, and while I don't feel compelled to begin the second book of the trilogy - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3588825.Lustrum__Trilogia_de_C_cero___2_" title="Lustrum (Trilogia de Cícero, #2) by Robert Harris" rel="nofollow noopener">Lustrum</a> - immediately, I will definitely be picking it up at some point in the future. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11521346334601682842" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_511714103" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/511714103" />
    <a title="Dean Lombardo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11112181-dean-lombardo"><img alt="Dean Lombardo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1519669516p2/11112181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/511714103?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dean Lombardo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dean Lombardo" href="/user/show/11112181-dean-lombardo">Dean Lombardo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743498661" href="/book/show/243607.Imperium">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer511714103" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17962873793822984499">Call it a prequel to Robert Harris' "Pompeii," if you like, "Imperium" follows the brilliant career of Roman senator, advocate and philosopher, Marcus Cicero as he outdazzles his political rivals in a decades-long display of words and wit, without having to resort to the threats and violence that his enemies do. The tale is narrated by Tiro, Cicero's scribe, who appparently really did exist, though his scrolls were lost. "Imperium" tries to recapture the content of those lost scrolls through Har</span>
  <span id="freeText17962873793822984499" style="display:none">Call it a prequel to Robert Harris' "Pompeii," if you like, "Imperium" follows the brilliant career of Roman senator, advocate and philosopher, Marcus Cicero as he outdazzles his political rivals in a decades-long display of words and wit, without having to resort to the threats and violence that his enemies do. The tale is narrated by Tiro, Cicero's scribe, who appparently really did exist, though his scrolls were lost. "Imperium" tries to recapture the content of those lost scrolls through Harris' usual research and writing talents. However, this historical ficiton novel lacks the intensity and eruptive forces of "Pompeii" (after all "Pompeii" occurred in the shadow of a tumescent Mount Vesuvius in 79 A.D. and featured more "in your face" conflict than did the more cerebral, gradual "Imperium" tale). I give "Imperium" four stars, and I recommend it -- and any historical novel from Harris -- to readers looking for historical fiction efficiently brought to life without the nauseating, make-your-eyes-bleed detail of yesteryear's historical novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17962873793822984499" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2996907561" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15882409738147837757">Imperium by Robert Harris, a work of historical fiction Cicero politician, lawyer, philosopher recounted by his slave Tiro during Rome's last days as a democratic republic. Cicero who came from a noble family but humble by the standards of the day, follows his ups and downs, a renowned orator involved in the numerous intrigues of Roman politics, and backed by Tirentia his staunchest supporter and his wife. Cicero constantly extolled politicians to return to former times of high morals, honesty, </span>
  <span id="freeText15882409738147837757" style="display:none">Imperium by Robert Harris, a work of historical fiction Cicero politician, lawyer, philosopher recounted by his slave Tiro during Rome's last days as a democratic republic. Cicero who came from a noble family but humble by the standards of the day, follows his ups and downs, a renowned orator involved in the numerous intrigues of Roman politics, and backed by Tirentia his staunchest supporter and his wife. Cicero constantly extolled politicians to return to former times of high morals, honesty, self sacrifice, reverence, this fictional account is told in a crime novel way with all its intrigues and possible traps and tricks, novel ends when he at his lowest point and no way he can head except up. You just have to forget your knowledge of Roman classics to make it suspenseful and enjoyable bit hard I know but not impossible.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15882409738147837757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('i1rd2JTpN+1+BVYHAKbMJW70y7fduNanc4wP0v3RyJL9P6LoQIWfs98j0GvRF2yq3ZaXkzRl+4hTasNXvQqe/g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8+YvhfUMB02A07hEsx29ejcjljuq6Mcmjw7hffr0JKGFg1C1IWCvEyH1PihirB31hEHKH0M16gmv6C34ui9yzQ==')})
  }

Tom Holland’s enthralling account tells the story of Caesar’s generation, <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6207660165396951631\" style=\"display:none\">In 49 B.C., the seven hundred fifth year since the founding of Rome, Julius Caesar crossed a small border river called the Rubicon and plunged Rome into cataclysmic civil war. Tom Holland’s enthralling account tells the story of Caesar’s generation, witness to the twilight of the Republic and its bloody transformation into an empire. From Cicero, Spartacus, and Brutus, to Cleopatra, Virgil, and Augustus, here are some of the most legendary figures in history brought thrillingly to life. Combining verve and freshness with scrupulous scholarship, Rubicon is not only an engrossing history of this pivotal era but a uniquely resonant portrait of a great civilization in all its extremes of Conn Iggulden is just such a writer, bringing to vivid life one of the most fascinating eras <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8791814894035985726\" style=\"display:none\">Rarely, if ever, does a new writer dazzle us with such a vivid imagination and storytelling, flawlessly capturing the essence of a land, a people, a legend. Conn Iggulden is just such a writer, bringing to vivid life one of the most fascinating eras in human history. In a true masterpiece of historical fiction, Iggulden takes us on a breathtaking journey through ancient Rome, sweeping us into a realm of tyrants and slaves, of dark intrigues and seething passions. What emerges is both a grand romantic tale of coming-of-age in the Roman Empire and a vibrant portrait of the early years of a man who would become the most powerful ruler on earth: Julius Caesar. On the lush Italian peninsula, a new empire is taking shape. At its heart is the city of Rome, a place of glory and decadence, beauty and bloodshed. Against this vivid backdrop, two boys are growing to manhood, dreaming of battles, fame, and glory in service of the mightiest empire the world has ever known. One is the son of a senator, a boy of privilege and ambition to whom much has been given and from whom much is expected. The other is a bastard child, a boy of strength and cunning, whose love for his adoptive family-and his adoptive brother-will be the most powerful force in his life. As young Gaius and Marcus are trained in the art of combat-under the tutelage of one of Rome\'s most fearsome gladiators-Rome itself is being rocked by the art of treachery and ambition, caught in a tug-of-war as two rival generals, Marius and Sulla, push the empire toward civil war. For Marcus, a bloody campaign in Greece will become a young soldier\'s proving ground. For Gaius, the equally deadly infighting of the Roman Senate will be the battlefield where he hones his courage and skill. And for both, the love of an extraordinary slave girl will be an honor each will covet but only one will win. The two friends are forced to walk different paths, and by the time they meet again everything will have changed. Both will have known love, loss, and violence. And the land where they were once innocent will be thrust into the grip of bitter conflict-a conflict that will set Roman against Roman...and put their friendship to the ultimate test. Brilliantly interweaving history and adventure, Conn Iggulden conjures a stunning array of contrasts-from the bloody stench of a battlefield to the opulence of the greatest city in history, from the tenderness of a lover to the treachery of an assassin. Superbly rendered, grippingly told, Emperor, The Gates of Rome is a work of vaulting imagination from a powerful new voice in historical fiction. &quot;From the Hardcover edition.&quot;<\/span>\n  <a <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5507625637508142406">ROBERT HARRIS is the author of nine best-selling novels: Fatherland, Enigma, Archangel, Pompeii, Imperium, The Ghost Writer, Conspirata, The Fear Index, and An Officer and a Spy. Several of his books have been adapted to film, most recently The Ghost Writer, directed by Roman Polanski. His work has been translated into thirty-seven languages. He lives in the village of Kintbury, England, with his </span>
  <span id="freeText5507625637508142406" style="display:none">ROBERT HARRIS is the author of nine best-selling novels: Fatherland, Enigma, Archangel, Pompeii, Imperium, The Ghost Writer, Conspirata, The Fear Index, and An Officer and a Spy. Several of his books have been adapted to film, most recently The Ghost Writer, directed by Roman Polanski. His work has been translated into thirty-seven languages.
